                .addSize([0, 0], [640, 480])
                .build()
        };

        gptPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.page ? digitalData.page.page : "blank";
        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

        googletag.pubads()
            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/smll.201802221')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

        googletag.defineSlot($GooglePublisherCategory, 'fluid', 'advert-leaderboard')
            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)
            .addService(googletag.pubads())
            .setCollapseEmptyDiv(true);

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="95c3b071c2964d68a23a58b539c37b2a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '05270445-c357-4f8b-8ced-db2fd526a0c3'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            <div class="accordion-tabbed"><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Yang/Wang" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a1" data-db-target-for="a1" aria-controls="a1" aria-haspopup="true" id="a1_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Wang Yang</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a1" aria-labelledby="a1_Ctrl" role="region" id="a1">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d114164632" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Carbon materials have attracted considerable attention as anodes for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries due to their low cost and environmental friendliness. This work reports an advanced carbon nanostructure that takes advantage of the chelation effect of glucose and metal ions, which ensures the uniform dispersion of metal in the precursor. Thus, an effective catalytic conversion from sp<sup>3</sup> to sp<sup>2</sup> carbon occurs, enabling simultaneously formation of pores with catalyzed graphitic structures. Due to the low carbonization temperature and short carbonization time as well as the different catalytic degree of various metals, a series of expanded graphitic layers from 0.34 to 0.44 nm with defects and amorphous carbon structure are obtained. The structure not only offers accessible graphitic spacings for reversible lithium/sodium ion insertion, but also provides abundant active sites for lithium/sodium ion adsorption in the defects and amorphous structure. Moreover, the hierarchical interconnected porous structure combining graphitic ribbons is beneficial for fast electronic/ionic transport and favorable electrolyte permeation. More importantly, such advanced carbon materials prove their feasibility for balancing the pore structure and degree of graphitization. When serving as the electrode material for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries, excellent electrochemical performance along with fast kinetics and long cycle life is achieved.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802221-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802221-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The excessive global depletion of nonrenewable fossil fuels makes it urgent to develop a clean and sustainable energy storage system. In this regard, rechargeable lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and other potential alternatives such as sodium ion batteries (SIBs) have promise as advanced energy storage devices, which have elicited tremendous research interest.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0001_R_d114164624e372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0005_R_d114164624e372" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></span> As for the anode materials, alloys and metal oxides/sulfides show much higher specific capacities for LIBs and SIBs, but the severe volume expansion and intrinsic poor electrical conductivity of these electrode materials still hinder their practical application.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0006_R_d114164624e374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Chem. - Asian J.">6</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0008_R_d114164624e374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></span> Carbon materials with abundant resources, low cost, nontoxicity, and environmental friendliness have been confirmed for the lithium and sodium storage, and extensive investigation has demonstrated that carbonaceous materials can become promising electrode candidates for LIBs and SIBs.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0009_R_d114164624e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0014_R_d114164624e376" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Carbon">14</a></span> Graphite, the commercialized carbonaceous anode material for LIBs, suffers two major stubborn disadvantages, low theoretical specific capacity (372 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and limited rate or cyclic capability in LIBs, due to its energy storage mechanism (the formation of a graphite intercalation compound of formula LiC<sub>6</sub>). Simple graphite is unsuitable for SIBs owing to its narrow interlayer distance and the larger sodium ionic radius, as well as the thermodynamic instability of Na–C binary compounds.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0015_R_d114164624e383" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></span> Therefore, it is crucial to develop an advanced carbon material with favorable nanostructure for efficient and stable lithium/sodium storage.</p>
         
         <p>Recently, various carbonaceous materials, including expanded graphite, carbon spheres, carbon nanofibers, hollow carbon nanowires, and biomass-derived carbon, have been investigated as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0016_R_d114164624e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nat. Commun.">16</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0024_R_d114164624e388" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></span> Quite interestingly, carbon materials exhibiting disordered nature with a low degree of graphitization can deliver a relatively high capacity, and are known as amorphous carbon or hard carbon (HC). Amorphous carbon mainly consists of disordered graphene layers and randomly packed microdomains. This structure provides nanosized pores and large interlayer space for ions storage and reversible insertion/desertion, thus resulting in a large capacity for LIBs and successful sodium ion storage.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0025_R_d114164624e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0030_R_d114164624e390" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Carbon">30</a></span> However, recent research mostly focused on the capacity advantage of HC while neglecting its defects of low electronic conductivity and poor stability ascribed to the disordered microarchitecture. Notably, the electrical conductivity of carbon material is considerably crucial, and is dependent on the degree of graphitization. However, the particularly obvious trade-off relation between amorphous and graphitic extent is a stubborn obstruction for constructing a perfect carbon architecture with superior ion storage capability and high electric conductivity simultaneously. Moreover, appropriate carbon structures such as hollow nanostructured carbon, porous carbon, carbon fibers, and carbon sheets have proved to be kinetically favorable for ionic and electronic transport.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0022_R_d114164624e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0031_R_d114164624e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nano Energy">31</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0037_R_d114164624e392" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nanoscale">37</a></span> Therefore, it is necessary to prepare the carbon electrode materials with a high degree of graphitization, suitable disordered as well as having a favorable secondary microstructure for achieving good energy storage capacity, high rate, and cycling performance. Accordingly, it is highly desirable to search for novel carbon materials possessing high electric conductivity and the optimum disordered structure.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, we propose a fundamental strategy to prepare an advanced carbon nanostructure under low carbonization temperature and short carbonization time, which exhibits improved alignment of carbon interlayers attributed to the appropriate introduction of two carbon components (ordered expanded nanographites and amorphous carbon micropores). In electrochemical reactions with electrolyte ions, the as-prepared carbon material enables stable storage through both interlayer intercalation (expanded nanographites) and interfacial adsorption (amorphous carbon micropores). Furthermore, the introduced graphitic ribbons provide more efficient electron transport. The carbon delivers a high capacity as well as an improved cycling stability for LIBs and SIBs. Such excellent lithium and sodium storage performances can be attributed to several nanostructural advantages: i) the interconnected graphite structure can expedite electronic transport and the expanded interlayer distance achieves reversible and stable ion insertion/desertion; ii) the defects in the graphite layers provide active sites for efficient ion adsorption; iii) the amorphous structure enhances ion storage; iv) the porous frameworks can accommodate volume expansion and stabilize the overall structure; v) the 3D hierarchical interconnected architecture can accelerate the electrolyte penetration and ion transportation.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802221-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802221-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201802221-sec-0030">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201802221-sec-0030-title">2.1 Materials Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>The general strategy for fabricating metal-catalyzed graphitic porous carbons (M-GPCs) is illustrated in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0001">1</a>. In a typical synthesis, glucose as the carbon source was thoroughly chelated with the metal ion in aqueous solution containing metal nitrate, acquiring a uniform distribution of M<sup>2+</sup> within the precursor architecture. After the annealing process under nitrogen atmosphere at elevated temperatures, M<sup>2+</sup> were transformed to metal oxide or metal in the carbon framework, which would serve as the mesoporous template and catalyst for graphitization. Meanwhile, the negative ions would constantly convert to abundant gases, which can act as activation regent to create plentiful micropores and defects in carbon matrix. The catalytic effect of the transition metal assists in partly transforming the sp<sup>3</sup> carbon atoms into the sp<sup>2</sup>. Particularly, as the carbonization temperature is not too high and the carbonization time is relatively short, thus leading to the expanded and defective graphitic layers. As expected, carbon material with a suitable amorphous structure balanced with the expanded graphitic layers was obtained and offers several advantages: First, the 3D interconnected graphitic ribbons with porous structure provide a favorable electronic and ionic transport network, and the expanded graphitic interlayer distance achieves reversible and stable ion insertion/desertion.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0036_R_d114164624e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a></span> Second, the defects in the graphite layers combine with the amorphous structure and nanovoids provide abundant active sites for efficient ion storage, thus improving capacity.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0026_R_d114164624e431" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ChemElectroChem">26</a></span> Third, the porous frameworks can accommodate the volume expansion of carbon layers and the graphitic ribbons with amorphous structure can stabilize the overall architecture during electrochemical cycling.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0033_R_d114164624e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Nano Energy">33</a></span> The above characteristics of M-GPCS make them feasible and stable for superior lithium/sodium ion storage capability.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f8154332-de85-4317-97fc-a83c81d8d1da/smll201802221-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f8154332-de85-4317-97fc-a83c81d8d1da/smll201802221-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/abe2d737-1624-4b50-b46c-a5058966bc06/smll201802221-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f8154332-de85-4317-97fc-a83c81d8d1da/smll201802221-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration showing the formation of M-GPCs for efficient ion storage and electron transfer.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>a–e depicts the transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the intermediate product of M-GPCs after carbonization without acid washing. It is observed that many nanoparticles with different sizes are embedded in the carbon matrix. After acid washing to remove the metal or metal oxide nanoparticles, the pore structure of M-PGCs can be observed distinctly in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>f–j. All these carbon samples show abundant porous structure owing to the nanoparticle templating. Nevertheless, the porous structures generated are slightly different from each other, ascribed to the difference of metal species. The same results can be obtained from the scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images (Figure S1, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e062708e-2c82-4ec9-b88b-451a6a6fc784/smll201802221-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e062708e-2c82-4ec9-b88b-451a6a6fc784/smll201802221-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/c1a609ab-29ef-46d5-b437-98fd4d1fa329/smll201802221-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e062708e-2c82-4ec9-b88b-451a6a6fc784/smll201802221-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–e) TEM image of the intermediate product after carbonization without acid washing: a) Mn; b) Fe; c) Co; d) Ni; e) Cu. f–j) TEM images of the final carbon samples: f) Mn-GPC; j) Fe-GPC; h) Co-GPC; i) Ni-GPC; j) Cu-GPC. k–o) XRD patterns of the precursors annealed at 300, 500, or 750 °C without acid washing and M-GPCs. p) Raman spectra of M-GPCs.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>To explore the formation mechanism of M-GPCs, X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectra of the intermediate products at different temperatures without acid washing and M-GPCs were obtained and are shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>k–o. Clearly, Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>k reveals that the dispersed manganese salt precursors have converted to MnO<sub>2</sub> nanoparticles at 300 °C, corresponding to PDF#06-0592. As the temperature increased, the intermediate product did not change. After washing by acid, we can see that there is a broad peak at 23° without graphitic species at 26°.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0038_R_d114164624e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">38</a></span> Therefore, Mn shows inactivity for catalytic graphitization in the investigated temperature range. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>l presents XRD patterns of the intermediate products of an iron salt during the carbonization process. When the annealed temperature reached 300 °C, the iron salt initially converted to Fe<sub>2</sub>(C<sub>2</sub>O<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub>, corresponding to PDF#11-0817. As the temperature rose to 500 °C, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles on amorphous carbon were formed, corresponding to PDF#11-0614. The Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were then reduced to bcc Fe(0) (PDF#06-0696) accompanied by the appearance of iron carbide peaks (Fe<sub>3</sub>C:PDF#34-0001), when the temperature was raised further to 750 °C. Consequently, Fe-GPC with partially catalyzed graphitization was observed with the appearance of the d(002) graphitic peak at 2θ = 26° besides the broad peak at 23°. The catalytic effect may be divided into two parts: First, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is reduced by carbon, the resultant Fe(0) is combined with amorphous carbon to form Fe<sub>3</sub>C. Due to the instability of Fe<sub>3</sub>C, a decomposition reaction can occur; thus Fe(0) and graphitized carbon formed. Similarly, Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>m reveals that a cobalt salt first changed into CoO corresponding to PDF#48-1719 and then reduced to fcc Co (PDF#15-0806) at 500 °C. When the temperature was raised to 750 °C, the intensity of the characteristic peak of Co enhanced, but no other obvious diffraction peaks were observed. Moreover, the d(002) graphite reflection at about 25.5° appeared with a broad peak at 23°, while less pronounced intensity than in the case of Fe. We noted that there exists a characteristic peak of Co<sub>2</sub>C (PDF#43-1144) coinciding with Co. Although the decomposition temperature of Co<sub>2</sub>C is near 485 °C, Co<sub>2</sub>C was not yet fully decomposed at 500 °C attributed to the unique structure. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>n presents XRD patterns of the intermediate products of a nickel salt during the carbonization process. It is found that the nickel salt preliminarily transformed into NiO (PDF#47-1049) and then reduced to Ni (PDF#04-0850). Similarly, the characteristic peaks of NiC coinciding with Ni were also observed, indicating that NiC (PDF#14-0020) was involved in its formation. The Ni nanoparticles are capable of capturing carbon atoms from the amorphous carbon to form metastable NiC. Because graphitic carbon is thermodynamically more stable than NiC, graphite-like carbon shells will subsequently segregate on the surface of the Ni particles. As can be seen from the XRD patterns of Co-GPC, the characteristic peak of graphite at about 25.3° appeared along with a broad peak at 23°. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>o, the dispersed copper salt precursors have been reduced to fcc Cu nanoparticles (PDF#04-0836) at 300 °C. In the temperature range of 300–750 °C, there is no evidence of formation of CuO, and only a broad diffraction peak of amorphous carbon at 23° was observed.</p>
            
            <p>Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0002">2</a>p displays Raman spectra of M-GPCs. It is noted that M-GPCs show a typical D (defect) band at around 1345 cm<sup>−1</sup> and a G (graphite) band at around 1580 cm<sup>−1</sup>. The D-band is assigned to the disordered or defective structures in the carbon matrix, while the G band can be attributed to the E<sub>2g</sub> symmetric vibration of sp<sup>2</sup>-bonded carbon atoms.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0039_R_d114164624e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0040_R_d114164624e543" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Carbon">40</a></span> The intensity ratios of <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> are 1.26, 1.06, 1.12, 1.13, and 1.16 for Mn-GPC, Fe-GPC, Co-GPC, Ni-GPC, and Cu-GPC, respectively. The above results confirm an elevated degree of catalytic graphitization role that Fe &gt; Co ≈ Ni &gt; Cu &gt; Mn, which coincides with the intensity of the d(002) diffraction peak in XRD patterns. The thermal stability is also one of the important parameters of the carbon material, which has a certain correlation with the degree of graphitization. Therefore, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) analysis was studied to further estimate the thermal stability and graphitization degree of M-GPCs under air atmosphere. As can be seen in Figure S2a (Supporting Information), the samples exhibit different resistance to the oxidation in air. The oxidation occurs around 370, 350, 310, 300, and 290 °C for Fe-GPC, Ni-GPC, Co-GPC, Cu-GPC, and Mn-GPC, respectively. Clearly, Fe-GPC exhibits the most resistance to oxidation, whereas there are no significant differences with the rest of the M-GPCs. This phenomenon can be related to the relative degree of graphitization, which is in accordance with the XRD and Raman results. Moreover, Raman mapping was conducted as shown in Figure S2b (Supporting Information). The M-C-D refers to the D band of samples, and M-C-G corresponds to the G band. The mapping figures provide further evidence of the coexistence of amorphous and graphitized carbon structure. The electronic conductivity of M-GPCs also was tested at 7 MP (Figure S2c, Supporting Information), Fe-GPC exhibited the highest value, which is consistent with its degree of graphitization.</p>
            
            <p>The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images in <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0003">3</a> clearly demonstrate the nanostructure of M-GPCs. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0003">3</a>a–d shows that Mn-GPC mainly presents an amorphous structure with abundant micropores, and only few short-range ordered expanded interlayer distances of about 0.42 nm can be found. On the contrary, Fe-GPC mostly exhibits ordered graphitic layers with a thickness of about 5–10 nm. Owing to the high degree of catalyzed graphitization by Fe, the interlayer distance of 0.34 nm is very close to the ideal graphite. It can be seen that some graphite microcrystal stripes are discontinuous, and some graphitic layers are crisscrossed with each other in the ordered nanodomains, giving rise to defects such as edges and pores, which will provide sufficient active sites for Li<sup>+</sup> storage. Co-GPC and Ni-GPC deliver similar expanded interlayer distances of 0.36–0.38 nm because of a relatively weak catalytic degree than Fe. Notably, these two samples offer several structural advantages: first, the expanded interlayer distance is accessible for lithium and sodium insertion; second, Co-GPC/Ni-GPC possesses defects in the graphitic structure and shows crisscrossed void channels which can enhance lithium and sodium storage; third, the concomitant amorphous carbon and micropores are regard as active sites for ion adsorption; fourth, the hierarchical interconnected structure surrounding the large mesopores and the smaller mesopores provides a rapid ionic and electronic transport network and simultaneously ensures structural integrity and minimal volume expansion during cycling.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0034_R_d114164624e562" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a></span> It is noteworthy that Cu-GPC seems to be catalyzed differently, and its graphitic structure is different from that of Mn-GPC. At the interface of carbon and Cu, the carbon is somehow ordered with an expanded interlayer distance of 0.44 nm, and the thickness is about 5 nm. This may be attributed to Cu acting as a growth substrate and the carbon layers can arrange along the Cu particle surface and form a particular carbon nanostructure. In addition, amorphous carbon and abundant micropores can be observed. As a result, metal salts can be seen acting as a graphitization catalyst, but also playing an important role in the formation of pores.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3047342c-8e91-4ab8-b4e4-f17737ebf815/smll201802221-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3047342c-8e91-4ab8-b4e4-f17737ebf815/smll201802221-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/03e539a8-af59-4675-ba72-28284a95f633/smll201802221-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3047342c-8e91-4ab8-b4e4-f17737ebf815/smll201802221-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">HRTEM images of M-GPCs. a–d) Mn-GPC. e–h) Fe-GPC. i–l) Co-GPC. m–p) Ni-GPC. q–t) Cu-GPC.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p><b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0004">4</a> presents N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms and the pore size distributions of M-GPCs. According to the International Union of Pure and Applied Chemistry (IUPAC) classification, the significant N<sub>2</sub> uptake at very low relative pressure is accordance with type I(a), indicating the presence of some micropores generated from the carbonization process.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0041_R_d114164624e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Pure Appl. Chem.">41</a></span> It can be clearly seen that the micropore portion is relatively low in Mn-GPC, Co-GPC, Cu-GPC compared to Fe-GPC and Ni-GPC. With the increase in relative pressure, the nitrogen adsorption is accompanied by a hysteresis belonging to type IV(a), implying the existence of mesopores. Furthermore, at high relative pressure, the curve shows unrestricted adsorption up to high <i>p</i>/<i>p</i><sub>0</sub>, which matches well with type ΙΙ and can be indicative of a macropore distribution. The nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms further confirm the hierarchical structure of M-GPCs. The pore size distributions of the M-GPCs are presented in the insets of Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0004">4</a> and the corresponding pore textures are summarized in Table S1 (Supporting Information). The M-GPCs possess a moderate Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area (SSA) ranging from 505 to 1234 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Undoubtedly, most of the SSA was contributed by mesopores with an average pore diameter of 10–20 nm. These results are in good agreement with TEM images and also demonstrate that Mn-GPC with the highest specific surface area and porosity cannot catalyze the graphitization of the sample, while Fe-GPC with the smaller specific surface area showed the best catalytic graphitization. This intriguing phenomenon might be explained by Fe being capable of arranging carbon atoms in a more orderly fashion, thus leading to the decrease in porosity and specific surface area, which demonstrates the competing effects between the pore structure and the degree of graphitization. Moreover, nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms of the samples without acid washing have been characterized and the corresponding specific surface area and porosity have been provided in the revised manuscript (Figure S3 and Table S1, Supporting Information). From the results, all the samples show low BET surface area and pore volume, and the most contribution is from micropores. This can demonstrate the significant role of the metal compounds. The surface structure may have a great influence on its electrochemical performance. Therefore, high-resolution X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) of M-GPCs have been tested and shown in Figure S4 and Table S2 (Supporting Information). All the samples present a predominant C1s peak at 284.5 eV and O1s peak at 532 eV. The XPS spectrum of C1s (Figure S5, Supporting Information) and O1s (Figure S6, Supporting Information) confirm the oxygen functional groups containing C<span class="icomoon"></span>O and CO.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0024_R_d114164624e603" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Carbon">24</a></span></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9ab1a87d-f389-4ab9-90fc-3e10acd4d337/smll201802221-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/9ab1a87d-f389-4ab9-90fc-3e10acd4d337/smll201802221-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0c2dc7b3-99c8-4f45-bcf2-0766652a7354/smll201802221-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9ab1a87d-f389-4ab9-90fc-3e10acd4d337/smll201802221-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Nitrogen adsorption–desorption isotherms of M-GPCs (the inset is corresponding pore size distribution).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll201802221-sec-0040">
            
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="smll201802221-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Performances</h3>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">The electrochemical performance of M-GPCs as anodes for LIBs was investigated in a voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0 V. <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>a shows the galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves of M-GPCs at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. We can see that the Mn-GPC and Cu-GPC mainly manifest an adsorption behavior for lithium storage. However, Cu-GPC slightly shows an insertion feature at low voltage, which can be ascribed to the ordered expanded interlayers of 0.44 nm.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0022_R_d114164624e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></span> Conversely, Fe-GPC presents a prominent insertion characteristic over other samples ascribed to its approximate ideal graphite structure, thus delivering a relatively limited specific capacity. Nevertheless, Co-GPC and Ni-GPC exhibit a combined lithium storage adsorption and insertion property.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0025_R_d114164624e639" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Energy Storage Mater.">25</a></span> Their curve characteristics are similar, but Co-GPC shows a slightly higher specific capacity. It can be seen that Co-GPC exhibits the highest specific capacity, thus the detailed storage mechanism of Co-GPC was analyzed in the following experiments. The first two charge–discharge curves of Co-GPC were shown in Figure S7a (Supporting Information), the initial coulombic efficiency is 57.6%. The capacity loss during the first cycle results from the formation of the solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) film as well as the irreversible reaction between lithium and surface oxygen functional groups.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0012_R_d114164624e641" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Carbon">12</a></span> GCD curves of Co-GPC at different current densities are demonstrated in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>b, it exhibits impressive electrochemical performance and very small IR drop (ohmic drop of the solution) even at high current loads, demonstrating the excellent charge transfer kinetics of the nanostructure of Co-GPC. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements at different scan rates were performed to further understand the electrochemistry of the Co-GPC anode, as shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>c, which exhibits battery insertion behavior at 0.01 V and capacitive hybrid features at higher potentials. For carbon materials, the common lithium/sodium storage modes are insertion and adsorption.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0015_R_d114164624e648" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></span> The Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the enlarged graphitic layers is a diffusion-controlled intercalation process (DIP). The adsorption of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> onto the nanovoids and surface defects/functional groups has been called the surface induced capacitive process (SCP).<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0022_R_d114164624e659" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Nano Lett.">22</a></span> As previously reported, the contribution from DIP or SCP can be evaluated through cyclic voltammetry at various scan rates by Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201802221-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">1</a><span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0015_R_d114164624e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0042_R_d114164624e662" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802221-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/c38798cf-5802-46d6-9341-707467f6b141/smll201802221-math-0001.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802221:smll201802221-math-0001" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802221:smll201802221-math-0001" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>i</i> is the measured current, ν is the scan rate, and <i>a, b</i> are adjustable parameters. when <i>b</i> is close to 0.5, the main contribution of <i>i</i> comes from the DIP, and if <i>b</i> approaches 1, the SCP is dominant. The current values at three potentials of 0.01, 0.75, and 1.5 V were utilized for the <i>i</i> values in the equation. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>d depicts the linear relationship between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>, and the corresponding <i>b</i> value is 0.61 (ν ≤ 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.01 V, indicating that the capacity contribution at the peak voltage is dominated by the intercalation process. Interestingly, when increasing the scan rate from 1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the <i>b</i> value becomes 0.57. The relative invariability of DIP at higher scan rates is mainly due to the intercalation in the expanded graphitic layers, which can support the fast electrochemical processes at a high scan rate. The result further proves the stability of the nanostructured carbon anode. The <i>b</i> values at 0.75 and 1.5 V are 0.91 and 0.93 at a scan rate of ≤1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which is much higher than 0.5, suggesting that the <i>i</i> includes a large contribution from SCP. Continuing to increase the scan rate from 1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the <i>b</i> values at 0.75 and 1.5 V are 0.83 and 0.82, respectively, revealing that the SCP still dominates. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>e presents the contribution ratios between the two different processes (DIP and SIP) at various scan rates, which can be calculated by Equation <a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#smll201802221-disp-0002" title="Link to equation">2</a><span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0042_R_d114164624e709" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Energy Environ. Sci.">42</a></span><div class="inline-equation" id="smll201802221-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><img class="" src="/cms/asset/9e4d1bb1-b1d7-484d-95c1-c467b106a72b/smll201802221-math-0002.png" alt="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802221:smll201802221-math-0002" title="urn:x-wiley:dummy:media:smll201802221:smll201802221-math-0002" loading="lazy"></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>where <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>), <i>k</i><sub>1</sub>ν, <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>ν<sup>0.5</sup>, and ν represent the current at a fixed voltage, the capacitive and diffusion-controlled currents, and scan rate, respectively. The regions of diffusion-controlled and capacitive contributions in the CV curve at a scan rate of 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were calculated to further determine the storage mechanism for Co-GPC anode. The capacitive ratio (shadow area) accounts for 50.8% of the total area, which implies that the high capacitive contribution to the total capacity leads to the good capability of Co-GPC anode at a high current density. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>f, the Co-GPC anode could exhibit reversible capacities of 950, 702, 599, 500, 405, 358, and 300 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 0.1, 0.5, 1, 3, 5, 10, and 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, Co-GPC anodes exhibited a long cycling performance due to their capacitive feature, delivering a reversible capacity of ≈945 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a reversible capacity of 493 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0005">5</a>g,h).</div>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f38cebeb-b1b2-477d-b5f8-80b78f0cb6d0/smll201802221-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f38cebeb-b1b2-477d-b5f8-80b78f0cb6d0/smll201802221-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/1b783315-2f65-4d28-867d-a6ee658a6ecd/smll201802221-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f38cebeb-b1b2-477d-b5f8-80b78f0cb6d0/smll201802221-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of M-GPCs as anodes for LIBs. a) GCD curves of M-GPCs at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD curves of Co-GPC at different current densities from 0.2 to 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) CV curves of Co-GPC at different scan rates from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Linear relationships between log <i>i</i> and log ν at 0.01, 0.42, and 1.5 V in the low scan rate region (left) and the relatively high scan rate region (right). e) Separation of storage contribution from the capacitance and diffusion-controlled process at 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (the former is marked with stripes). f) Rate performance of Co-GPC at different current densities. g,h) Cycling performance at current densities of g) 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and h) 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of M-GPCs as an anode for SIBs was also conducted in a voltage range from 0.01 to 3.0 V. <b>Figure</b><a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>a shows the GCD curves of M-GPCs at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. We can see that Mn-GPC and Fe-GPC mainly manifest an adsorption behavior for sodium storage. Nevertheless, Cu-GPC, Co-GPC, and Ni-GPC exhibit a combined sodium insertion storage and adsorption property. Their curve characteristics are similar and the Cu-GPC show a slightly higher specific capacity. Thus, the detailed storage mechanism of Cu-GPC was further analyzed in the following experiments. The first two charge–discharge curves of Cu-GPC were shown in Figure S9a (Supporting Information), the initial coulombic efficiency is 56.1%. Similar with the LIBs, the capacity loss during the first cycle also results from the formation of the SEI film as well as the side reaction between sodium and surface functional groups.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0043_R_d114164624e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">43</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0046_R_d114164624e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Mater.">46</a></span> GCD curves of Cu-GPC at different current densities are demonstrated in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>b, it exhibits a relatively small IR drop even at high currents, demonstrating excellent charge transfer kinetics. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>c displays the CV curves of a Cu-GPC anode at different scan rates and battery/capacitive hybrid characteristics are observed. Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>d depicts the linear relationship between log <i>i</i> and log <i>v</i>, and the corresponding <i>b</i> value is 0.51 (ν ≤ 1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>) at 0.01 V, indicating that the capacity contribution at the peak voltage is almost completely by the intercalation process. When at higher scan rates of 1–10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the <i>b</i> value is 0.74, suggesting the combined sodium storage of adsorption and insertion. The <i>b</i> values at 0.42 and 1.5 V are 0.91 and 0.75, respectively, at a scan rate of ≤1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than 0.5, suggesting that the <i>i</i> includes a large contribution of SCP. At the higher scan rate range of 1–10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>, the <i>b</i> values at 0.42 and 1.5 V are 0.79 and 0.69, respectively, revealing the coexistence of SCP and DIP processes and further confirming the stability of this carbon structure. The regions of diffusion-controlled and capacitive contributions in the CV curve at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> were calculated to further determine the storage mechanism for Cu-GPC anodes. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>e, the capacitive area (marked with stripes) accounted for 55.3% of the total area. The other unmarked area in the CV loop could be attributable to the Na-ion reversible insertion and extraction from the stacked graphene layers of Cu-GPC. The high capacitive contribution to the total capacity leads to the good capability of Cu-GPC anode at high current density. As shown in Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>f, Cu-GPC anode exhibited reversible capacities of 330 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, and still retained a high specific capacity of 165 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, Cu-GPC anodes exhibit good cycling stability due to their capacitive feature, delivering a reversible capacity of 328 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a reversible capacity of 197 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a href="#smll201802221-fig-0006">6</a>g,h). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were carried out before cycling and after 200 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, as shown in Figures S12 and S13 (Supporting Information), all samples show a smaller high frequency semicircle than that of in fresh cells, demonstrating stable Li/Na-ion diffusivity during cycling.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0023_R_d114164624e844" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">23</a></span> Moreover, SEM images at lithiation/sodiation states and after the cycling tests were shown in Figures S14 and S15 (Supporting Information). The structure of Co-GPC and Cu-GPC can still be well preserved for LIBs and SIBs anodes, respectively. These result further confirm the stability of this carbon nanostructure for lithium or sodium ion storage.<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0047_R_d114164624e846" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Joule">47</a></span> The pure carbon directly carbonized from glucose shows very low specific capacity and poor rate performance (Figures S16 and S17, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="smll201802221-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a4edb973-9db0-4473-b83d-6cd800df5fc4/smll201802221-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a4edb973-9db0-4473-b83d-6cd800df5fc4/smll201802221-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/96a24dae-3589-482b-853a-ecc042aa57ee/smll201802221-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a4edb973-9db0-4473-b83d-6cd800df5fc4/smll201802221-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201802221-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201802221" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of M-GPCs as anodes for SIBs. a) GCD curves of M-GPCs at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) GCD curves of Cu-GPC at different current densities from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>. c) CV curves of Cu-GPC at different scan rates from 0.1 to 10 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. d) Linear relationships between log <i>i</i> and log ν at 0.01, 0.42, and 1.5 V in the low scan rate region (left) and the relatively high scan rate region (right). e) Separation of storage contribution from the capacitance and diffusion-controlled process at 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> (the former is marked with stripes). f) Rate performance of Cu-GPC at different current densities. g,h) Cycling performance at current densities of g) 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and h) 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>The above characterization results demonstrate the advanced features of Co-GPC for lithium storage and Cu-GPC for sodium storage, such as the hierarchically porous structure, interconnected expanded graphitic layers, defects, and amorphous carbon, as well as nanovoids. These structures can synergistically enhance stable energy storage by: i) the interconnected expanded graphite structure expediting electronic transport and achieving reversible and stable ion insertion/desertion; ii) the defects in the graphite layers, the surrounding amorphous carbon as well as nanovoids providing abundant active sites for efficient ion adsorption; iii) 3D hierarchical interconnected porous frameworks accelerating the electrolyte penetration, accommodating volume expansion, and stabilizing the overall carbon structure during cycling. Hence, the graphitic carbon materials in this work show good performance among other reported ones (Table S3, Supporting Information).<span><a href="#smll201802221-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0004_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0011_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Carbon">11</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0013_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nanoscale">13</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0017_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Storage Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0019_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">19</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0020_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A﻿﻿">20</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0034_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0036_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Nano">36</a>, <a href="#smll201802221-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0043_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">43</a>-<a href="#smll201802221-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201802221-bib-0046_R_d114164624e883" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Adv. Mater.">46</a></span> It is noteworthy that, apart from except Co-GPC exhibiting best lithium storage and Cu-GPC exhibiting best sodium storage, Ni-GPC also delivered good energy storage behavior for both lithium and sodium anodes. As can be seen from Figures S7d and S9e (Supporting Information), the GCD curves also show a combined capacitor and battery behavior for both lithium and sodium anodes. Ni-GPC also exhibits very stable sodium storage properties after long cycles (Figure S11, Supporting Information), which can be attributed to its accessible graphitic distance (≈0.37 nm) for sodium ion insertion. Additionally, M-GPC materials (M = Fe, Ni, Co, Cu, Mn) also performed well for supercapacitor electrodes especially in high rate performance (Figures S18 and S19, Supporting Information), which can be ascribed to the fast ionic/electronic pathway in these advanced carbon structures.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802221-sec-0050">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802221-sec-0050-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, M-GPCs as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs have been successfully prepared. When used as anode material for LIBs, the unique structural features of Co-GPC endowed it with excellent electrochemical performance and a relatively stable capacity of 950 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Moreover, it still delivers a reversible capacity of 945 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and a reversible capacity of 493 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 3000 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Cu-GPC also exhibits stable cyclic performance when used as an anode material for SIBs, and it shows capacity of 328 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 197 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2000 cycles at 3 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The superior properties of M-GPCs can be attributed to the synergistic effects associated with the hierarchical interconnected graphitic ribbons with amorphous carbon, defects, pores, nanovoids, and an expanded interlayer distance (≈0.36 nm for Co-GPC and ≈0.44 nm for Cu-GPC), which allows stable and reversible lithium/sodium ion adsorption and insertion, and simultaneously provides fast ionic/electric pathway. We believe that the M-GPCs electrode materials with this advanced carbon nanostructure and stable energy storage properties can be promising in high performance LIBs/SIBs and other energy storage devices.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201802221-sec-0060">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201802221-sec-0060-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Transition Metal-Catalyzed Graphitic Porous Carbon</i>: The transition metal-catalyzed graphitic porous carbons were prepared via a preliminary chelation process followed by carbonization at high temperature. Typically, 1 g glucose was dissolved in 5 mL of deionized water to form solution A. 1 mmol metal nitrate (manganese nitrate, ferric nitrate, cobalt nitrate, nickel nitrate, or copper nitrate) was dispersed in 5 mL deionized water to form solution B. Subsequently, solution B was added dropwise to A with vigorous stirring to form a homogeneous solution. Then the solution was put into an oven and heated at 120 °C to evaporate water and obtain the carbon precursor. Afterward, the powder was annealed in tube furnace under nitrogen at 750 °C for 1 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. The obtained intermediate products were then washed with 15 wt% HCl solution to remove the metal oxide or metal. Finally, the samples were collected by vacuum filtration, washed with abundant distilled water/ethanol, and dried at 80 °C in oven overnight. The carbon samples were named as M-GPC, where M refers to manganese, ferrum, cobalt, nickel, or copper, respectively.</p>
         
         <p><i>Material Characterization</i>: The structure of M-GPCs and the intermediate products during the carbonization process were measured by XRD on a Rigaku SmartLab X-ray diffractometer operated at 40 kV with Cu Kα radiation, and the scan rate was 5° min<sup>−1</sup>. The morphologies and carbon nanostructure of M-GPCs were observed by field emission scanning electron microscopy (Hitachi S-4800, Japan) and HRTEM (JEOL-2100F). The Raman spectra were tested on a Horiva (Xplora Plus) at an excitation wavelength of 532 nm to analyze the degree of graphitization. Nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms were measured by a Micromeritics V-Sorb 2800P analyzer at 77 K. The surface area was calculated by the BET method. Pore size distribution was obtained by the Saito–Foley method and Barrett–Joyner–Halenda method. The electric conductivity of M-GPCs was measured by ST2722-SZ Semiconductor powder resistivity instrument under 7 MPa.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The electrochemical measurements were carried out with 2032 type coin cells at room temperature. Typically, M-GPCs, carbon black, and polyvinylidene fluoride were mixed in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 in N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone to form a homogeneous slurry. Then, the slurry was continuously coated on copper foil, and dried at 80 °C in a vacuum oven for 12 h. The mass loading of each electrode was ≈1.5 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. For LIBs, lithium foil was used as the counter electrode, the electrolyte was 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate, diethyl carbonate, and dimethyl carbonate (1:1:1 by volume), and a Celgard-2400 membrane was used as a separator. For SIBs, Na metal was used as a counter electrode, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in a mixture of ethylene carbonate and propylene carbonate (2:1 by volume) was used as an electrolyte, and glass fiber membranes were used as a separator. The cells were assembled in an argon-filled glovebox with water and oxygen contents of less than 0.1 ppm. The GCD measurements were recorded on a NEWARE auto-cycler, and the potential window was 0.01 to 3 V. CV measurements were conducted on a VMP3 (ProDigitek, Australia).</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
